# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Rust Macro Expansion to HIR Lowering Chain

## Your Task

Trace how Rust procedural macros and declarative macros are expanded and lowered to HIR in rust-lang/rust. Find Rust source files implementing: the macro expander entry point, the token stream transformation, the AST expansion, and the HIR lowering from expanded AST.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the crossrepo domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7, sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7` (rust-lang/rust)
- `sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99` (servo/servo)

## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings in the following structure:

```json
{
  "files": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go"}
  ],
  "symbols": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "SymbolName"}
  ],
  "chain": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "FunctionName"}
  ],
  "text": "Narrative explanation of your findings, citing repos and file paths."
}
```

Include only the fields relevant to this task. Your answer is evaluated against a closed-world oracle — completeness matters.

- **File recall and precision**: Did you find all relevant files?

{"content":"1: //! Lowers the AST to the HIR.\n2: //!\n3: //! Since the AST and HIR are fairly similar, this is mostly a simple procedure,\n4: //! much like a fold. Where lowering involves a bit more work things get more\n5: //! interesting and there are some invariants you should know about. These mostly\n6: //! concern spans and IDs.\n7: //!\n8: //! Spans are assigned to AST nodes during parsing and then are modified during\n9: //! expansion to indicate the origin of a node and the process it went through\n10: //! being expanded. IDs are assigned to AST nodes just before lowering.\n11: //!\n12: //! For the simpler lowering steps, IDs and spans should be preserved. Unlike\n13: //! expansion we do not preserve the process of lowering in the spans, so spans\n14: //! should not be modified here. When creating a new node (as opposed to\n15: //! \"folding\" an existing one), create a new ID using `next_id()`.\n16: //!\n17: //! You must ensure that IDs are unique. That means that you should only use the\n18: //! ID from an AST node in a single HIR node (you can assume that AST node-IDs\n19: //! are unique). Every new node must have a unique ID. Avoid cloning HIR nodes.\n20: //! If you do, you must then set the new node's ID to a fresh one.\n21: //!\n22: //! Spans are used for error messages and for tools to map semantics back to\n23: //! source code. It is therefore not as important with spans as IDs to be strict\n24: //! about use (you can't break the compiler by screwing up a span). Obviously, a\n25: //! HIR node can only have a single span. But multiple nodes can have the same\n26: //! span and spans don't need to be kept in order, etc. Where code is preserved\n27: //! by lowering, it should have the same span as in the AST. Where HIR nodes are\n28: //! new it is probably
... (truncated)

For example `· a $( a )* a b` is one, as is `a $( · a )* a b`.\n19: //!\n20: //! ## Summary

The answer documents the complete pipeline for Rust procedural and declarative macro expansion followed by AST to HIR lowering:

### Key Components Found:

1. **Macro Expansion Entry Point** (`rustc_expand/src/expand.rs`):
   - `MacroExpander::expand_crate()` - Main entry point
   - `expand_invoc()` - Dispatcher for individual macro invocations

2. **Procedural Macro Expansion** (`rustc_expand/src/proc_macro.rs`):
   - `BangProcMacro::expand()` - Executes proc macros via the proc-macro bridge

3. **Declarative Macro Expansion** (`rustc_expand/src/mbe/`):
   - Pattern matching: `TtParser` (NFA-based parser in `macro_parser.rs`)
   - Expansion: `expand_macro()` in `macro_rules.rs`
   - Token transformation: `transcribe()` in `transcribe.rs`

4. **Token Stream to AST** (`rustc_expand/src/expand.rs`):
   - `parse_ast_fragment()` - Converts token streams to AST fragments

5. **AST to HIR Lowering** (`rustc_ast_lowering/src/`):
   - `lower_to_hir()` - Entry point
   - `ItemLowerer` - Walks expanded AST and lowers to HIR
   - Specific lowering for items, expressions, patterns

The answer includes detailed descriptions of each file and symbol, plus a complete execution chain showing how data flows through each stage of the compilation process.

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: //! This is an NFA-based parser, which calls out to the main Rust parser for named non-terminals\\n2: //! (which it commits to fully when it hits one in a grammar). There's a set of current NFA threads\\n3: //! and a set of next ones. Instead of NTs, we have a special case for Kleene star. The big-O, in\\n4: //! pathological cases, is worse than traditional use of NFA or Earley parsing, but it's an easier\\n5: //! fit for Macro-by-Example-style rules.\\n6: //!\\n7: //! (In order to prevent the pathological case, we'd need to lazily construct the resulting\\n8: //! `NamedMatch`es at the very end. It'd be a pain, and require more memory to keep around old\\n9: //! matcher positions, but it would also save overhead)\\n10: //!\\n11: //! We don't say this parser uses the Earley algorithm, because it's unnecessarily inaccurate.\\n12: //! The macro parser restricts itself to the features of finite state automata. Earley parsers\\n13: //! can be described as an extension of NFAs with completion rules, prediction rules, and recursion.\\n14: //!\\n15: //! Quick intro to how the parser works:\\n16: //!\\n17: //! A \\\"matcher position\\\" (a.k.a. \\\"position\\\" or \\\"mp\\\") is a dot in the middle of a matcher, usually\\n18: //! written as a `\u00b7`. For example `\u00b7 a $( a )* a b` is one, as is `a $( \u00b7 a )* a b`.\\n19: //!\\n20: //! The parser walks through the input a token at a time, maintaining a list\\n21: //! of threads consistent with the current position in the input string: `cur_mps`.\\n22: //!\\n23: //! As it processes them, it fills up `eof_mps` with threads that would be valid if\\n24: //! the macro invocation is now over, `bb_mps` with threads that are waiting on\\n25: //! a Rust non-terminal like `$e:expr`, and `next_mps` with threads that are waiting\\n26: //! on a particular token. Most of the logic concerns moving the \u00b7 through the\\n27: //! repetitions indicated by Kleene stars. The rules for moving the \u00b7 without\\n28: //! consuming any input are called epsilon transitions. It only advances or calls\\n29: //! out to the real Rust parser when no `cur_mps` threads remain.\\n30: //!\\n31: //! Example:\\n32: //!\\n33: //! ```text, ignore\\n34: //! Start parsing a a a a b against [\u00b7 a $( a )* a b].\\n35: //!\\n36: //! Remaining input: a a a a b\\n37: //! next: [\u00b7 a $( a )* a b]\\n38: //!\\n39: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - -\\n40: //!\\n41: //! Remaining input: a a a b\\n42: //! cur: [a \u00b7 $( a )* a b]\\n43: //! Descend/Skip (first position).\\n44: //! next: [a $( \u00b7 a )* a b]  [a $( a )* \u00b7 a b].\\n45: //!\\n46: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - -\\n47: //!\\n48: //! Remaining input: a a b\\n49: //! cur: [a $( a \u00b7 )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\\n50: //! Follow epsilon transition: Finish/Repeat (first position)\\n51: //! next: [a $( a )* \u00b7 a b]  [a $( \u00b7 a )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\\n52: //!\\n53: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - - (this looks exactly like the last step)\\n54: //!\\n55: //!     "content": "1: //! This is an NFA-based parser, which calls out to the main Rust parser for named non-terminals\n2: //! (which it commits to fully when it hits one in a grammar). There's a set of current NFA threads\n3: //! and a set of next ones. Instead of NTs, we have a special case for Kleene star. The big-O, in\n4: //! pathological cases, is worse than traditional use of NFA or Earley parsing, but it's an easier\n5: //! fit for Macro-by-Example-style rules.\n6: //!\n7: //! (In order to prevent the pathological case, we'd need to lazily construct the resulting\n8: //! `NamedMatch`es at the very end. It'd be a pain, and require more memory to keep around old\n9: //! matcher positions, but it would also save overhead)\n10: //!\n11: //! We don't say this parser uses the Earley algorithm, because it's unnecessarily inaccurate.\n12: //! The macro parser restricts itself to the features of finite state automata. Earley parsers\n13: //! can be described as an extension of NFAs with completion rules, prediction rules, and recursion.\n14: //!\n15: //! Quick intro to how the parser works:\n16: //!\n17: //! A \"matcher position\" (a.k.a. \"position\" or \"mp\") is a dot in the middle of a matcher, usually\n18: //! written as a `\u00b7`. For example `\u00b7 a $( a )* a b` is one, as is `a $( \u00b7 a )* a b`.\n19: //!\n20: //! The parser walks through the input a token at a time, maintaining a list\n21: //! of threads consistent with the current position in the input string: `cur_mps`.\n22: //!\n23: //! As it processes them, it fills up `eof_mps` with threads that would be valid if\n24: //! the macro invocation is now over, `bb_mps` with threads that are waiting on\n25: //! a Rust non-terminal like `$e:expr`, and `next_mps` with threads that are waiting\n26: //! on a particular token. Most of the logic concerns moving the \u00b7 through the\n27: //! repetitions indicated by Kleene stars. The rules for moving the \u00b7 without\n28: //! consuming any input are called epsilon transitions. It only advances or calls\n29: //! out to the real Rust parser when no `cur_mps` threads remain.\n30: //!\n31: //! Example:\n32: //!\n33: //! ```text, ignore\n34: //! Start parsing a a a a b against [\u00b7 a $( a )* a b].\n35: //!\n36: //! Remaining input: a a a a b\n37: //! next: [\u00b7 a $( a )* a b]\n38: //!\n39: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - -\n40: //!\n41: //! Remaining input: a a a b\n42: //! cur: [a \u00b7 $( a )* a b]\n43: //! Descend/Skip (first position).\n44: //! next: [a $( \u00b7 a )* a b]  [a $( a )* \u00b7 a b].\n45: //!\n46: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - -\n47: //!\n48: //! Remaining input: a a b\n49: //! cur: [a $( a \u00b7 )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\n50: //! Follow epsilon transition: Finish/Repeat (first position)\n51: //! next: [a $( a )* \u00b7 a b]  [a $( \u00b7 a )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\n52: //!\n53: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - - (this looks exactly like the last step)\n54: //!\n55: //! Remaining input: a b\n56: //! cur: [a $( a \u00b7 )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\n57: //! Follow epsilon transition: Finish/Repeat (first position)\n58: //! next: [a $( a )* \u00b7 a b]  [a $( \u00b7 a )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\n59: //!\n60: //! - - - Advance over an a. - - - (this looks exactly like the last step)\n61: //!\n62: //! Remaining input: b\n63: //! cur: [a $( a \u00b7 )* a b]  [a $( a )* a \u00b7 b]\n64: //! \"## Rust Macro Expansion to HIR Lowering Chain\\n\\nThe Rust compiler's macro expansion and HIR lowering process follows a multi-stage pipeline:\\n\\n### Stage 1: Compilation Pipeline Orchestration\\nThe compilation starts in `rustc_interface/src/passes.rs` which sets up the macro expansion configuration and initiates the process by calling `ExtCtxt::monotonic_expander().expand_crate(krate)`.\\n\\n### Stage 2: Macro Expansion Engine\\nThe main expansion logic resides in `rustc_expand/src/expand.rs`. The `MacroExpander` struct orchestrates the entire process:\\n\\n1. **`expand_crate()`** - Entry point that recursively expands all macro invocations in the crate\\n2. **`fully_expand_fragment()`** - Recursively processes AST fragments by collecting macro invocations and replacing them with placeholders, then expanding them\\n3. **`expand_invoc()`** - Dispatcher for individual macro invocations, routing to either procedural or declarative macro handlers based on the `SyntaxExtensionKind`\\n\\n### Stage 3: Procedural Macro Expansion\\nProcedural macros are handled in `rustc_expand/src/proc_macro.rs`:\\n- `BangProcMacro::expand()` - Takes a token stream input and executes the procedural macro code through the proc-macro bridge\\n- `AttrProcMacro::expand_with_safety()` - Handles attribute-like procedural macros\\n\\nThe proc-macro server in `rustc_expand/src/proc_macro_server.rs` provides the runtime bridge for executing external proc-macro code.\\n\\n### Stage 4: Declarative Macro Expansion\\nDeclarative macros (\\\"macro by example\\\") are implemented in `rustc_expand/src/mbe/`:\\n\\n1. **Pattern Matching** (`macro_parser.rs`):\\n   - `TtParser` implements an NFA-based parser that matches macro input against the LHS patterns\\n   - Uses epsilon transitions and Kleene star handling to match token sequences\\n\\n2. **Macro Expansion** (`macro_rules.rs`):\\n   - `MacroRulesMacroExpander::expand()` - Entry point for declarative macros\\n   - `expand_macro()` - Core expansion function that:\\n     - Calls `try_match_macro()` to match input against defined rules\\n     - If matched, calls `transcribe()` to transform the matched tokens\\n\\n3. **Token Stream Transformation** (`transcribe.rs`):\\n   - `transcribe()` - Transforms the matched token patterns by:\\n     - Substituting matched variables into the RHS\\n     - Expanding Kleene repetitions (`*`, `+`, `?`)\\n     - Applying proper spacing and delimiter handling\\n   - `TranscrCtx` - Context manager that maintains the transcription state and result stack\\n\\n### Stage 5: Token Stream to AST Conversion\\nAfter macro expansion, the resulting token stream is converted to AST:\\n\\n1. **`MacroExpander::parse_ast_fragment()`** - Creates a parser from the token stream\\n2. **`parse_ast_fragment()` function** - Parses tokens into appropriate AST fragment types (Items, Stmts, Exprs, etc.)\\n3. **Placeholder Expansion** - Expanded AST is incorporated back into the original AST by replacing placeholder nodes\\n\\n### Stage 6: AST to HIR Lowering\\nOnce all macros are expanded, the AST is lowered to HIR in `rustc_ast_lowering/`:\\n\\n1. **`lower_to_hir()`** (`lib.rs`) - Entry point that:\\n   - Creates an `ItemLowerer` for traversing the expanded AST\\n   - Iterates through all definition IDs and lowers each one\\n\\n2. **Item-Level Lowering** (`item.rs`):\\n   - `ItemLowerer` - Walks through AST items and lowers them to HIR\\n   - `lower_item()` - Lowers individual items\\n   - `lower_node()` - Lowers a specific AST node to HIR\\n\\n3. **Expression and Pattern Lowering**:\\n   - `expr.rs` - Lowers various expression types\\n   - `pat.rs` - Lowers pattern types\\n   - `block.rs` - Lowers block-level constructs\\n\\n### Key Data Structures:\\n- **Token Stream**: Sequence of tokens produced by macros\\n- **AST Fragment**: Expanded AST nodes (Items, Stmts, Exprs, etc.)\\n- **HIR**: Final intermediate representation used for type checking and later compilation stages\\n- **NamedMatch**: Captured subexpressions during macro pattern matching\\n\\nThe entire process maintains proper hygiene information (via `SyntaxContext` and expansion IDs) to ensure correct name resolution and proper error reporting throughout the pipeline.\"\n}\n",

\"## Rust Macro Expansion to HIR Lowering Chain\\n\\nThe Rust compiler's macro expansion and HIR lowering process follows a multi-stage pipeline:\\n\\n### Stage 1: Compilation Pipeline Orchestration\\nThe compilation starts in `rustc_interface/src/passes.rs` which sets up the macro expansion configuration and initiates the process by calling `ExtCtxt::monotonic_expander().expand_crate(krate)`.\\n\\n### Stage 2: Macro Expansion Engine\\nThe main expansion logic resides in `rustc_expand/src/expand.rs`. The `MacroExpander` struct orchestrates the entire process:\\n\\n1. **`expand_crate()`** - Entry point that recursively expands all macro invocations in the crate\\n2. **`fully_expand_fragment()`** - Recursively processes AST fragments by collecting macro invocations and replacing them with placeholders, then expanding them\\n3. **`expand_invoc()`** - Dispatcher for individual macro invocations, routing to either procedural or declarative macro handlers based on the `SyntaxExtensionKind`\\n\\n### Stage 3: Procedural Macro Expansion\\nProcedural macros are handled in `rustc_expand/src/proc_macro.rs`:\\n- `BangProcMacro::expand()` - Takes a token stream input and executes the procedural macro code through the proc-macro bridge\\n- `AttrProcMacro::expand_with_safety()` - Handles attribute-like procedural macros\\n\\nThe proc-macro server in `rustc_expand/src/proc_macro_server.rs` provides the runtime bridge for executing external proc-macro code.\\n\\n### Stage 4: Declarative Macro Expansion\\nDeclarative macros (\\\"macro by example\\\") are implemented in `rustc_expand/src/mbe/`:\\n\\n1. **Pattern Matching** (`macro_parser.rs`):\\n   - `TtParser` implements an NFA-based parser that matches macro input against the LHS patterns\\n   - Uses epsilon transitions and Kleene star handling to match token sequences\\n\\n2. **Macro Expansion** (`macro_rules.rs`):\\n   - `MacroRulesMacroExpander::expand()` - Entry point for declarative macros\\n   - `expand_macro()` - Core expansion function that:\\n     - Calls `try_match_macro()` to match input against defined rules\\n     - If matched, calls `transcribe()` to transform the matched tokens\\n\\n3. **Token Stream Transformation** (`transcribe.rs`):\\n   - `transcribe()` - Transforms the matched token patterns by:\\n     - Substituting matched variables into the RHS\\n     - Expanding Kleene repetitions (`*`, `+`, `?`)\\n     - Applying proper spacing and delimiter handling\\n   - `TranscrCtx` - Context manager that maintains the transcription state and result stack\\n\\n### Stage 5: Token Stream to AST Conversion\\nAfter macro expansion, the resulting token stream is converted to AST:\\n\\n1. **`MacroExpander::parse_ast_fragment()`** - Creates a parser from the token stream\\n2. **`parse_ast_fragment()` function** - Parses tokens into appropriate AST fragment types (Items, Stmts, Exprs, etc.)\\n3. **Placeholder Expansion** - Expanded AST is incorporated back into the original AST by replacing placeholder nodes\\n\\n### Stage 6: AST to HIR Lowering\\nOnce all macros are expanded, the AST is lowered to HIR in `rustc_ast_lowering/`:\\n\\n1. **`lower_to_hir()`** (`lib.rs`) - Entry point that:\\n   - Creates an `ItemLowerer` for traversing the expanded AST\\n   - Iterates through all definition IDs and lowers each one\\n\\n2. **Item-Level Lowering** (`item.rs`):\\n   - `ItemLowerer` - Walks through AST items and lowers them to HIR\\n   - `lower_item()` - Lowers individual items\\n   - `lower_node()` - Lowers a specific AST node to HIR\\n\\n3. **Expression and Pattern Lowering**:\\n   - `expr.rs` - Lowers various expression types\\n   - `pat.rs` - Lowers pattern types\\n   - `block.rs` - Lowers block-level constructs\\n\\n### Key Data Structures:\\n- **Token Stream**: Sequence of tokens produced by macros\\n- **AST Fragment**: Expanded AST nodes (Items, Stmts, Exprs, etc.)\\n- **HIR**: Final intermediate representation used for type checking and later compilation stages\\n- **NamedMatch**: Captured subexpressions during macro pattern matching\\n\\nThe entire process maintains proper hygiene information (via `SyntaxContext` and expansion IDs) to ensure correct name resolution and proper error reporting throughout the pipeline.\"\n}\n",
